        <p class="date">
         Sat Jul 05, 2014 at 09:44 PM PDT
        </p>
        <h2>
         <a href="/story/2014/07/06/1312002/-California-s-Highway-Patrol-Stoop-to-New-Low-Pummelling-a-Possibly-Mentally-Ill-Great-Grandmother" id="titleHref">
          California's Highway Patrol Stoop to New Low. Pummelling a Possibly Mentally Ill Great-Grandmother.
         </a>
         <span style="white-space: nowrap">
         </span>
        </h2>
        <p class="author">
         <span>
          by
         </span>
         <a href="/user/jpmassar">
          jpmassar
         </a>
         <a alt="Follow this author" class="ajax type-user do-hotlist do-updateContent size-tiny off altTooltip" href="#?friend_id=155698&amp;is_stream=1">
          Follow
         </a>
        </p>
        <ul class="front-page-share clearfix">
         <li style="margin-left: 0px">
          <div class="fb-share-button" data-href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/07/06/1312002/-California-s-Highway-Patrol-Stoop-to-New-Low-Pummelling-a-Possibly-Mentally-Ill-Great-Grandmother" data-layout="button_count">
          </div>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="twitter-share-button" data-count="horizontal" data-related="markos" data-text="California's Highway Patrol Stoop to New Low. Pummelling a Possibly Mentally Ill Great-Grandmother." data-url="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/07/06/1312002/-California-s-Highway-Patrol-Stoop-to-New-Low-Pummelling-a-Possibly-Mentally-Ill-Great-Grandmother" data-via="dailykos" href="https://twitter.com/share">
           Tweet
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="comments comment_count_with_new" data-comment_count="139" data-story_id="1312002" href="/story/2014/07/06/1312002/-California-s-Highway-Patrol-Stoop-to-New-Low-Pummelling-a-Possibly-Mentally-Ill-Great-Grandmother#comments">
           139 Comments
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </div>
       <div class="article-body">
        <div id="intro">
         <p>
          You may have seen
          <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/07/03/1311471/--Now-You-re-Going-to-Jail-Cop-Quote-After-Tipping-Over-Parapalegic-in-Wheelchair">
           the video of the Indiana cop shoving a paraplegic, tipping him over from the curb onto the street
          </a>
          . That was bad enough.
         </p>
         <p>
          You may even have already seen the video of the incident alluded to in the title, where a California Highway Patrolman mercilessly beats a woman by the side of the freeway, which came out about the same time. Here it is if you haven't.
         </p>
         <center>
          <iframe defang_allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0" height="280" src="//www.youtube.com/embed/DcCN5C7UBx0" width="480">
          </iframe>
          <br/>
         </center>
         <p>
          But what we didn't know until now was much of anything about the woman.
          <a href="http://www.latimes.com/local/lanow/la-me-ln-chp-altercation-20140705-story.html">
           It's coming out bit by bit in the Los Angeles Times
          </a>
          , and I think we can honestly say CHP has stooped to a new low.
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          "She's not just some animal," said attorney Caree Harper, who declined to name the woman. "She has an aunt, a sister, a brother, a father and a
          <b>
           great-grandchild
          </b>
          ."
          <p>
           ...
          </p>
          <p>
           The woman is being housed at a mental health facility at Martin Luther King Jr. Community Hospital in South Los Angeles and doctors are not allowing the family to see her, Harper said.
          </p>
         </blockquote>
         The woman seems to have been found walking in traffic by the CHP officer who then began beating her repeatedly, swinging his fists into her head. CHP claims, almost miraculously, that she was not hurt. I have my doubts, but here's the quote:
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          CHP officials told reporters at a Friday news conference that she was not injured and that she was undergoing a mental health evaluation.
         </blockquote>
         What of the CHP
         <s>
          officer
         </s>
         monster? He hasn't been identified. He's been put on paid leave. The family's attorney did say
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          "There is no justification for the way that he savagely beat her. He's the one that should be in a mental health facility... We are not going to let this woman be on trial. We're going to turn the attention on the wannabe MMA [mixed martial arts] cop who repeatedly punched her as she was lying on her back."
         </blockquote>
         The District Attorney will no doubt refuse to prosecute him (if in fact charges are not pressed against the woman for "resisting arrest."). The DA will conclude that the use of force was justified.
         <p>
          There will be a lawsuit. The State of California will probably have to pay some significant amount of money. Only because of the existence of the video, of course.
         </p>
         <p>
          And the officer? He'll likely keep his job - you know, the one they advertise about prowling California's highways for great-grandmothers to beat the living shit out of.
          <br/>
         </p>
        </div>
        <p class="divider-doodle">
        </p>
        <div class="article-body" id="body">

       <form action="/ajax/story/setTags" class="do-validate on-popup-do-reload" method="POST">
        <input name="story_id" type="hidden" value="1312002"/>
        <style>
         #popup-story_setTags div.content-bar p { font-size: 13px; text-align: left; }
	#popup-story_setTags div.content-bar a			{ color: #E5721E }
	#popup-story_setTags div.content-bar a:hover,
	#popup-story_setTags div.content-bar a:focused,
	#popup-story_setTags div.content-bar a:visited	{ color: #7C470C }
        </style>
        <br/>
        <div>
         <p>
          Add keywords that describe this diary.
		Separate multiple keywords with commas.
          <br/>
          <a href="/special/tag_tips" target="_blank">
           Tagging tips
          </a>
          -
          <a href="/search?search_tags=1" target="_blank">
           Search For Tags
          </a>
          -
          <a href="http://www.dkosopedia.com/wiki/Tag:Tagfinder" target="_blank">
           Browse For Tags
          </a>
         </p>
         <div class="pageTip hasTooltip" style="margin-top:3px">
          ?
         </div>
         <div class="tooltipContents qtip-style-dk4_large qtip-pos-br">
          <p>
           <b>
            More Tagging tips:
           </b>
          </p>
          <p>
           A tag is a way to search for this diary. If someone is searching for "Barack Obama," is this a diary they'd be trying to find?
          </p>
          <p>
           Use a person's full name, without any title. Senator Obama may become President Obama, and Michelle Obama might run for office.
          </p>
          <p>
           If your diary covers an election or elected official, use election tags, which are generally the state abbreviation followed by the office. 
		CA-01 is the first district House seat. CA-Sen covers both senate races. NY-GOV covers the New York governor's race.
          </p>
          <p>
           Tags do not compound: that is, "education reform" is a completely different tag from "education". A tag like "reform" alone is probably not meaningful.
          </p>
         </div>
         <!-- &lt;h4 id="p_tagErrorMessage"&gt;You must enter at least one Tag for this Diary Entry:&lt;/h4&gt; -->
         <p>
          <input id="p_tagInput" name="tag_draft" size="70" type="text" value=""/>
          <!-- &lt;input id="tagSubmit" type="submit" name="submit" value="Submit" /&gt; -->
         </p>
         <p style="font-style:italic">
          Consider if one or more of these tags fits your diary:
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Civil Rights');return false;">
           Civil Rights
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Community');return false;">
           Community
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Congress');return false;">
           Congress
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Culture');return false;">
           Culture
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Economy');return false;">
           Economy
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Education');return false;">
           Education
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Elections');return false;">
           Elections
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Energy');return false;">
           Energy
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Environment');return false;">
           Environment
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Health Care');return false;">
           Health Care
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('International');return false;">
           International
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Labor');return false;">
           Labor
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Law');return false;">
           Law
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Media');return false;">
           Media
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Meta');return false;">
           Meta
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('National Security');return false;">
           National Security
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Science');return false;">
           Science
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Transportation');return false;">
           Transportation
          </a>
          , or
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('White House');return false;">
           White House
          </a>
          .
	If your diary is specific to a state, consider adding the state 
	(
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('California');return false;">
           California
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Texas');return false;">
           Texas
          </a>
          , etc).
	Keep in mind, though, that there are many wonderful and important diaries
	that don't fit in any of these tags. Don't worry if yours doesn't.
         </p>

           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             police officers as one of my parents worked at the local police station.
            </p>
            <p>
             I remember many good officers that were kind and were very dedicated to their communities. Protect and Serve was a good was to describe what they did.
            </p>
            <p>
             But I saw plenty of the other side. Sometimes I was involved with police officers doing things that I know they personally had arrested many people for doing the exact thing. There were even a few officers that ended up on the other side of the jail cell door.
            </p>
            <p>
             I think the training and culture of the police has taken a turn for the worse in the last couple decades and the whole concept of protect and serve has been bastardized and now refers to corporate interests instead of the people.
            </p>
           </div>

            <p class="sig">

              <li id="c89">
               <div class="cx" id="cx53868882">
                <h5>
                 <a class="de">
                 </a>
                 <span class="cu">
                  There is an interesting concept
                 </span>
                 <span class="crd ntb">
                  (
                  <a href="/comments/1312002/53868882#c89?mode=alone;showrate=1#c89">
                   5+ / 0-
                  </a>
                  )
                 </span>
                </h5>
                <div class="crs">
                 <dl>
                  <dt>
                   Recommended by:
                  </dt>
                  <dd>
                   <a href="/user/uid:141635">
                    greengemini
                   </a>
                   ,
                   <a href="/user/uid:155698">
                    jpmassar
                   </a>
                   ,
                   <a href="/user/uid:54189">
                    reflectionsv37
                   </a>
                   ,
                   <a href="/user/uid:985214">
                    chrisculpepper
                   </a>
                   ,
                   <a href="/user/uid:1000367">
                    raspberryberet
                   </a>
                  </dd>
                 </dl>
                </div>
                <div class="ct">
                 <p>
                  called "Collective Responsibility".
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  The idea is that all sections of the Executive have ownership of all of the decisions. That gives them both the right and responsibility to speak out.
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  If, for example, the EPA thinks that a policy of the State Department will cause problems, then they should say so.
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  Clearly on a matter of national concern, police brutality, everyone in authority should regard it as part of their public duty, and their job, to offer a view.
                 </p>
                </div>

            I appreciate the time and effort you take
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1312002/53865621#c8?mode=alone;showrate=1#c8">
             14+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>

           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:155698">
              jpmassar
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:194838">
              Santa Susanna Kid
             </a>

             <a href="/user/uid:165648">

             </a>

             <a href="/user/uid:93912">

             </a>

             </a>

              old mule
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:70396">
              AoT
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:145163">
              lilsky
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:67231">
              myboo
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:336064">
              GrannyRedBird
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:896132">
              VPofKarma
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:53021">
              viral
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:108040">
              cville townie
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            to keep us informed of this and other police-initiated outrages. I do share your outrage - HOWEVER I am concerned about your last three paragraphs, with their jaded implications that nothing ever can or will be done. I get that that's part of the outrage and frustration talking, but can we stop and think a minute about the message you're sending? Do you really want to send your readers on a path that leads to apathy and learned helplessness?
           </p>
           <p>
            Granted, I don't have a magic solution at hand, but I do know if we give up before we begin, nothing will ever change. At the least, if you have links to any kind of action alert - even if it's a petition or a fund to help defray the family's costs - please post them. Or ask if readers can provide such links. Taking action empowers us to take more action, far more effectively than assuming "nothing will be done."
           </p>
           <p>
            Another thing I'd like to see - if anyone will take it on - is a more systematized collection of these incidents. Maybe a series of #CopFAIL diaries, similar to the #GunFail or Gun-Crazy series. Because these incidents are not limited to one state or one municipality or one police force. I think it may more effective to spur action by showing the overall pattern.
           </p>
           <p>
            Just some thoughts.
           </p>

            <div class="cx" id="cx53866009">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               You get burnt out and cynical,
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1312002/53866009#c22?mode=alone;showrate=1#c22">
                4+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:155698">
                 jpmassar
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:664339">
                 old mule
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:73842">
                 4Freedom
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:108040">
                 cville townie
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               And bitter, and might drink more,
              </p>
              <p>
               But you don't stop, ever, except, some times to take breaks, for santities sake,
              </p>
              <p>
               But, on one issue for example, that I have been fighting for, along with hundreds of thousands of others, victories have been few and fleeting, and the situation on the ground is a thousand times worse now, than it was when I started fighting for the issue.
              </p>
              <p>
               If they had done then, what they get away with now, there would have been mass riots, arson, bombs and targeted killings.
              </p>
              <p>
               Doing what they do now, in regards to that issue, would have been massively illegal,
              </p>
              <p>
               Now, it's Government and Regulatory policy.
              </p>
              <p>
               You get burnt out and cynical, because in most cases, nothing is done, and in more cases than not, when Governmental or Regulatory change is made, it's not to protect the victim, (pick you issue), but to legalize the victimization.
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">
              </p>
              <p class="cb">
               by

                 <a href="/user/uid:155698">
                  jpmassar
                 </a>
                 ,
                 <a href="/user/uid:81371">
                  NancyWH
                 </a>
                </dd>
               </dl>
              </div>
              <div class="ct">
               <p>
                by the term "you," but I personally haven't had a drink in 13 years so it's not me. And I'm the last person to get burnt out and cynical.
               </p>
               <p>
                I know I expressed my concerns badly in my initial post, but it had to do with my concerns about the overall effect of reporting on an atrocity committed by public authorities, in some detail, and then concluding that, essentially, "nothing will done." Even IF that's historically it has counter effect on many people who take action.
               </p>
               <p>
                It strikes me the same way as comments on climate change diaries, the ones that say "we're fucked - it's too late - can't reverse it..." and I have to wonder how many people come away from that saying, okay, let's eat, drink &amp; be merry. If you can't change anything why bother ...
               </p>
               <p>
                Back to this particular, diary, perhaps the conclusion could have been expressed differently: "If history precedents hold true, this will not be prosecuted ... etc. etc. We can hope this case may be different." Or some such.
               </p>
               <p>
                I'm still expressing myself badly, so I will withdraw from this discussion.
               </p>
              </div>

          <li id="c25">
           <div class="cx" id="cx53866032">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              local petitions, not White House
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1312002/53866032#c25?mode=alone;showrate=1#c25">
               3+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:62260">
                peacestpete
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/user/uid:155698">
                jpmassar
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/user/uid:255072">
                jan4insight
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              The office of the President is
              <strong>
               far removed
              </strong>
              from authorities over local police departments and highway patrol. Petitioning the White House won't get anyone any action on what is a local issue.
             </p>
             <p>
              Want to make a difference? Have an impact? then it takes more than signing an online petition.
             </p>
             <p>
              In the case of highway patrol brutality, you'd probably want to write and call state legislators and the governor. In the case of local police department brutality, then call and write to council members and the mayor, and
              <strong>
               show up
              </strong>
              at city council meetings. Write letters to the editor, start your own petition and gather signatures.
             </p>
             <p>
              There is a pervasive misunderstanding that POTUS (any president) is some savior who can rise up and fix every problem in every corner of the nation. That is not how this government was designed to work.
             </p>
            </div>

         <li id="c124">
          <div class="cx" id="cx53878660">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             Fascinating, I think you and the author are
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1312002/53878660#c124?mode=alone;showrate=1#c124">
              1+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:972092">
               Krypto
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             both correct, there should be something we can do and doing something is better than doing nothing and so on, but, this isn't a political issue at the departmental point where the decisions generally get made; it is a police department and only occasionally a judicial issue.
            </p>
            <p>
             At some point the Prosecutor of this jurisdiction has to run for re-election and there might be some political leverage there. Since it is the State Patrol, there might be some leverage by petitioning Jerry Brown, but he may be reticent to get involved. If that is the case there is little real power the people have beyond taking video's that expose this kind of police brutality. One might petition the prosecutor but they are not supposed to respond to public pressure. If this is the case and the authorities decide not to prosecute, there is one other possibility.
            </p>
            <p>
             The only actual power is in long term organization to rid the county that this occurred in of the prosecutor if she or he does not bring charges against this officer. That's typically not something that most people are interested in pursuing because our cultural memory on these kinds of events usually doesn't last for several years. And that is why I think we all feel so helpless and frustrated regarding these events.
            </p>
            <p>
             Police kill innocent people in this country more often than we know. Our prosecutors bring charges against innocent people, our court system tries, convicts and sentences innocent people, and our prison's contain innocent people. Nicholas Kristof of
             <em>
              The New York Times
             </em>
             wrote Sunday of a man who was convicted of killing his wife because of prosecutorial misconduct. The prosecutor was eventually charged and convicted but the story is a real tear jerker. The man did twenty-five years for a murder he did not commit. And the real killer? He killed another woman nin nearly identical circumstances 18 months later.
            </p>
            <p>
             <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/06/opinion/sunday/nicholas-kristof-when-they-imprison-the-wrong-guy.html?partner=rss&amp;emc=rss">
              http://www.nytimes.com/...
             </a>
            </p>
           </div>

            <p>
             after any incident remotely like this BECAUSE of the
             <a href="http://www.policechiefmagazine.org/magazine/index.cfm?fuseaction=display_arch&amp;article_id=1512&amp;issue_id=62008">
              Known police preference for steroid use
             </a>
            </p>
            <blockquote>
             Although the traditional reason for the use of AASs is to improve athletic performance, AASs also appeal to officers wanting a tactical edge or an intimidating appearance. Unlike with other forms of drug abuse, steroid users do not take their drug recreationally; on the contrary, some state they need these drugs in order to do their job effectively or improve their job performance. From street officers who consider themselves vulnerable to bigger, more aggressive criminals to special-assignment officers who are regularly tested for their physical abilities, officers are turning to performance-enhancing drugs such as AASs and HGH as a shortcut to improved performance. This article will not delve into the abuse of HGH, which is not a controlled substance but is obtained by prescription only and has very limited usenone for normal adults.
            </blockquote>
            The article goes on to present "difficulties" in 'testing for illicit steroid use', a mindset I say is bullshit.
            <p>
             You can test for pot, test for steroids.
            </p>

             And rein in these out of control pigs.

          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            fact does not require fiction for
            <b>
             balance
            </b>
            (proudly a DFH)
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:26598">
             mollyd
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1312002/53867571#c58">
             Sun Jul 06, 2014 at 09:09:27 AM PDT

           <p class="cl">
           </p>

         </div>
        </li>
        <li id="c61">
         <div class="cx" id="cx53867652">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            In a former life I worked . . .
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1312002/53867652#c61?mode=alone;showrate=1#c61">
             7+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:336064">
              GrannyRedBird
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:155698">
              jpmassar
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:896132">
              VPofKarma
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:255072">
              jan4insight
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:81371">
              NancyWH
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:108040">
              cville townie
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:70753">
              deha
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            . . . for an architectural firm that had a national criminal justice practice (police stations, county jails, state and federal prisons). I participated in many police station designs in the early 90's.
           </p>
           <p>
            At that time, there was a push towards what was called "community policing". The previous rounds of police stations (built mostly in the 50's and 60's) were fortresses that kept the public at bay behind bullet proof glass and wire cages. You walked into a tiny, dark lobby and pushed a button for "service" and eventually a person would appear behind the glass to talk to you through an electronic squawk box.
           </p>
           <p>
            Community policing sought to soften the image a bit by putting welcoming lobbies into the front entrance to a police facility so that neighborhoods could see that the police were human as well. Some of the thought was to make the police less intimidating, and to thus encourage constituent participation in policing efforts.
           </p>
           <p>
            And almost every one of the designs I participate in had a state-of-the-art "health facility" that was replete with all of the latest body-building equipment. These were generally some of the areas of the facility that generated the most "buzz" among the agency. People really paid attention when the design of these spaces was discussed.
           </p>
           <p>
            Most of the upper ranks (say, lieutenant and above) were of the "protect and serve" variety, as well as many of the patrol officers. They knew the job was dangerous but that the danger was not overwhelming and ever-present (some areas obviously more dangerous than others).
           </p>
           <p>
            But there was always a group of unusually overdeveloped patrol officers who were both arrogant and aggressive. They seemed to feel that they were in a literal battle against the constituents they served. Think back to "Hill Street Blues", which ended in 1987. They were the Belkers and the Buntz.
           </p>
           <p>
            Though I didn't think of it at the time, I'm sure that some of these patrol officers made it into the upper echelons. Most police forces are represented by a union, and if you didn't get caught using too much force and passed the sergeant's exam you were probably going to be alright. Fast forward some years and we've got these guys in the administrative ranks, along with their combative attitudes.
           </p>
           <p>
            Then comes the guy out in California strolling down the street with the automatic weapons when the police had handguns and shotguns. Here's a clarion call to police across the nation, "arm up"! Put this together with the push towards "community policing", the arrogance and aggression that (maybe?) had infiltrated administrative ranks, the glamorization of violent actions by police in film and television, add in a dash of "tough on crime" pretenders on the city council and I think we have an almost perfect recipe for the "militarization" of police forces everywhere.
           </p>
           <p>
            I have the utmost respect for all members of the law enforcement community. I have worked closely with you. And I realize that you are expected to perform an absolutely thankless task on a shoestring budget. "Tough on crime" is, after all, just lip service for politicians who know that when they say it, it gets them votes, but who have little idea what is really necessary. However, none of the above excuses the behaviors we have seen lately.
           </p>
           <p>
            But I think the causes for the current increase in law enforcement brutality are myriad, the blame (hate that word) can be spread far and wide, and the solution is not going to be of the silver bullet variety. It starts with every one of us, law enforcement and constituents and politicians, realizing that it is not "us against them". It's just us against us. And we will always lose if we mistake some of "us" for "them".
           </p>
           <h1>
            
           </h1>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
           </p>

           <p>

           </p>
           <p>
            Hard to look at, but we need to see it, really see it.
           </p>
           <p>
            I worked with mentally ill people for eight years, mostly with severe schizophrenia. And have a close relative with the same illness.
           </p>
           <p>
            The idea that a cop would beat any of them up when what they need is help, is a horror story to me.
           </p>
           <p>
            The worst for those of us who work, or have worked, in this field is not being able to protect those in our care. And now we have to worry, not about criminals harming them (because they are vulnerable and easy to exploit), but in addition we have to worry about the police harming them. The very police who are supposed to protect and serve the public.
           </p>
           <p>
            It boggles the mind.

          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="tooltipContents qtip-style-dk4_large qtip-pos-br">

  <script type="text/javascript">
   if($.cookie('meter_user') !== 'false') {
		if ($('#ePostButton').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('#ePostButton').click(function() { 
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Posted Comment', { 
		      repliedTo: $(this).parents('#eP').prevAll('.cb').children('a').first().html() 
		    });
		  });
		}
		
		if (!!document.location.pathname.match('activate') &amp;&amp; $('h1:contains("Welcome to Daily Kos!")').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Signed Up');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname === '/') {
		  if (document.location.hostname.match(/^www|^dailykos/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Front Page');
		  } else {
		    var subdomain = document.location.hostname.split('.')[0];
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Vertical', { name: subdomain });
		  }
		}
		
		if ($('.ratinate').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.ratinate').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Comment') });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-addRating.type-story').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-addRating.type-story').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Story') });
		}
		
		if ($("form[action$='publishHow']").length &gt; 0) {
		  $(document).ready(function() {
			  $('.type-story.do-publishHow').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Published Story') });
			 });
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/user/) &amp;&amp; $('li.here').length &gt; 0) {
		  if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Stream/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Stream', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Profile/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Profile', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:last li.here').children('a').html().match(/My Recent Replies/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Replies');
		  }
		} 
		
		if ($('#search-tabbed').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Searched');
		}
		
		if ($('#group-tables').length &gt; 0) {
			DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Groups List');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/groups/) &amp;&amp; $("#group_info").length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Group', { name: $('.name').children('a').text() });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').click(function() {
		    var nickName;
		    nickName = ($(this).prev('a').html() || $('.name a').html())
		    if ($(this).hasClass('do-hotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Followed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    } else if ($(this).hasClass('do-unHotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Unfollowed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    }    
		  });
		}
	}
  </script>
